I've uploaded a code repository in the directory /testbed. Consider the following PR description:

<pr_description>
Error message for using the record spread syntax with positional-only records is unclear
Looks like an unintentional limitation of compiler (gleam 1.13.0/erlang 27.0).
Unpacking a record with only positional fields results in compilation failure "This is not a record constructor". This is only an issue for unlabelled records.

```gleam
// does not compile
type Positional{
    Positional(Int, Int)
}

pub fn pos_test(){
  let t1 = Positional(1,2)
  let t2 = Positional(..t1) // "This is not a record constructor"
  assert t1 == t2
}
```
`compiler-core/src/type_/expression.rs::3418..3432` returns `Error::RecordUpdateInvalidConstructor` since all-unlabelled record is missing `field_map`

Checking how it's related to #5195 

</pr_description>

  3332	    fn infer_record_update_variant<'c>(
  3333	        &mut self,
  3334	        constructor: &TypedExpr,
  3335	        value_constructor: &'c ValueConstructor,
  3336	        record: &TypedExpr,
  3337	    ) -> Result<RecordUpdateVariant<'c>, Error> {
  3338	        let record_type = record.type_();
  3339	        // The record constructor needs to be a function.
  3340	        let (arguments_types, return_type) = match constructor.type_().as_ref() {
  3341	            Type::Fn { arguments, return_ } => (arguments.clone(), return_.clone()),
  3342	            Type::Named { .. } | Type::Var { .. } | Type::Tuple { .. } => {
  3343	                return Err(Error::RecordUpdateInvalidConstructor {
  3344	                    location: constructor.location(),
  3345	                });
  3346	            }
  3347	        };
  3348	
  3349	        // It must be a record with a field map for us to be able to update it
  3350	        let (field_map, variants_count, variant_index, name) = match &value_constructor.variant {
  3351	            ValueConstructorVariant::Record {
  3352	                field_map: Some(field_map),
  3353	                variants_count,
  3354	                variant_index,
  3355	                name,
  3356	                ..
  3357	            } => (field_map, *variants_count, *variant_index, name.clone()),
  3358	            ValueConstructorVariant::Record {
  3359	                field_map: None, ..
  3360	            } => {
  3361	                return Err(Error::RecordUpdateInvalidConstructor {
  3362	                    location: constructor.location(),
  3363	                });
  3364	            }
  3365	            ValueConstructorVariant::LocalVariable { .. }
  3366	            | ValueConstructorVariant::ModuleConstant { .. }
  3367	            | ValueConstructorVariant::ModuleFn { .. } => {
  3368	                return Err(Error::RecordUpdateInvalidConstructor {
  3369	                    location: constructor.location(),
  3370	                });
  3371	            }
  3372	        };
  3373	
  3374	        // Check that the record type unifies with the return type of the constructor, and is
  3375	        // not some unrelated other type. This should not affect our returned type, so we
  3376	        // instantiate a new copy of the generic return type for our value constructor.
  3377	        let return_type_copy = match value_constructor.type_.as_ref() {

  3379	            Type::Named { .. } | Type::Var { .. } | Type::Tuple { .. } => {

  3381	                    location: constructor.location(),
  3382	                });
  3383	            }
  3384	        };

    61	                location,
    62	                context,
    63	                expected: self.arity as usize,

    65	            });

    70	                Some(_) => {
    71	                    labelled_arguments_given = true;
    72	                }
    73	
    74	                None => {

    32	pub struct Implementations {
    33	    /// Whether the function has a pure-gleam implementation.
    34	    ///
    35	    /// It's important to notice that, even if all individual targets are
    36	    /// supported, it would not be the same as being pure Gleam.
    37	    /// Imagine this scenario:
    38	    ///
    39	    /// ```gleam
    40	    /// @external(javascript, "wibble", "wobble")
    41	    /// @external(erlang, "wibble", "wobble")
    42	    /// pub fn func() -> Int
    43	    /// ```
    44	    ///
    45	    /// `func` supports all _current_ Gleam targets; however, if a new target
    46	    /// is added - say a WASM target - `func` wouldn't support it! On the other
    47	    /// hand, a pure Gleam function will support all future targets.
    48	    pub gleam: bool,
    49	    pub can_run_on_erlang: bool,
    50	    pub can_run_on_javascript: bool,
    51	    /// Whether the function has an implementation that uses external erlang
    52	    /// code.
    53	    pub uses_erlang_externals: bool,
    54	    /// Whether the function has an implementation that uses external javascript
    55	    /// code.
    56	    pub uses_javascript_externals: bool,
    57	}
    58	
    59	impl Implementations {
    60	    pub fn supporting_all() -> Self {
    61	        Self {
    62	            gleam: true,
    63	            can_run_on_erlang: true,
    64	            can_run_on_javascript: true,
    65	            uses_javascript_externals: false,
    66	            uses_erlang_externals: false,
    67	        }
    68	    }
    69	}
    70	
    71	/// The purity of a function.
    72	///
    73	/// This is not actually proper purity tracking, rather an approximation, which
    74	/// is good enough for the purpose it is currently used for: warning for unused
    75	/// pure functions. The current system contains some false negatives, i.e. some
    76	/// cases where it will fail to emit a warning when it probably should.

    78	/// If we wanted to properly track function side effects - say to perform
